This virus disease once it gets into the rose generally will kill your plant and unfortunately it appears that mosy roses are susceptible although there needs to be more research to confirm this. The only control at this point is to eliminate the virus from the area around your garden (eliminate all wild Rosa multiflora and any plants with symptoms) and to control the mite vector (the agent that spreads it from plant to plant) with miticides. This mite vector is a very small mite that moves by floating on air currents. See the below link for more information.\n\nThe \"What To Tell Your Customers About Downy Mildew And Rose Rosette Disease\" is located at \nhttp://www.todaysgardencenter.com/article/33127/what-to-tell-your-customers-about-downy-mildew-and-rose-rosette-disease", "icon": "https://fbstatic-a.akamaihd.net/rsrc.php/v2/yD/r/aS8ecmYRys0.gif", "type": "link", "id": "107207969434854_195772510578399", "status_type": "shared_story", "description": "The two diseases may impact your spring sales. Retailers must have a plan in place to address customer questions. Here\u2019s what you need to know."}

It is transmitted by a very small eriophyid mite which spreads by riding air currents. The virus appears to also transmit readily by grafting - including root grafting. Mechanical transmission does not appear to be a major factor in its transmission between roses. One approach to control its spread would be to control the mite vector. Once the virus has spread throughout the plant the plant will die although it has been suggested that if you cut a recently infected cane off at its base you may be able to prevent the virus from infecting the rest of the plant. \n\nThe Missouri Botanical Garden has some good pictures of the rose rosette symptoms.\n\nhttp://www.missouribotanicalgarden.org/gardens-gardening/your-garden/help-for-the-home-gardener/advice-tips-resources/pests-and-problems/diseases/viruses/rose-rosette.aspx", "type": "photo", "id": "107207969434854_178204955668488", "status_type": "added_photos", "icon": "https://fbstatic-a.akamaihd.net/rsrc.php/v2/yz/r/StEh3RhPvjk.gif"}

{"picture": "https://fbexternal-a.akamaihd.net/safe_image.php?d=AQB3QRtto60VTGsc&w=154&h=154&url=http%3A%2F%2Fagrilifecdn3.tamu.edu%2Fwp-content%2Fthemes%2FAgriLife-News%2Fimages%2FAgriLife-default-post-image.png", "from": {"category": "Education", "name": "Texas A&M Rose Breeding and Genetics Program", "id": "107207969434854"}, "name": "AgriLife Today | News and updates about Texas A&M AgriLife", "privacy": {"value": ""}, "actions": [{"link": "https://www.facebook.com/107207969434854/posts/296953633747636", "name": "Comment"}, {"link": "https://www.facebook.com/107207969434854/posts/296953633747636", "name": "Like"}], "updated_time": "2012-10-18T02:00:52+0000", "caption": "today.agrilife.org", "link": "http://today.agrilife.org/", "likes": {"paging": {"cursors": {"after": "NTY4NjE2NTM2", "before": "NTY4NjE2NTM2"}}, "data": [{"id": "568616536", "name": "Fabiola Byrne"}]}, "created_time": "2012-10-18T02:00:52+0000", "message": "COLLEGE STATION \u2014 Economic and social transformations worldwide are changing the way plants are grown and marketed to consumers, and retailers will have to adjust to survive, according to a leading horticulture association executive.\n\n\u201cEven with advanced technology and knowledge, you still have to look up and pay attention to what is happening around you,\u201d said Michael V. Geary, chief executive officer of OFA\u2014The Association of Horticulture Professionals.\n\nGeary spoke to researchers, business owners, students, teachers and other professionals as the keynoter for the Ellison Chair in International Floriculture Distinguished Lecture Series Oct. 17.\n\nHe said the multi-billion-dollar floriculture industry has to focus on its two main components:  production and retail.\n\n\u201cIn short, we develop plant products, and then we sell them,\u201d he said. \u201cHow do we keep from missing out on the opportunity to sell more plants, flowers, trees and related products? Many think one of the problems we have is the perceived disconnect between the growers and the retailers, and ultimately the consumers. Either way, we need to make sure we do not become obsolete.\u201d\n\nPart of the change for the industry comes from demographics, he said.\n\n\u201cThe retirement of the Boomers, the emerging Y Generation, and the X generation stuck in the middle all have different buying patterns and the outlook is not good for us, at least for several years, perhaps a decade or so,\u201d Geary said. \u201cBut we can\u2019t just sit around and wait for the size of the population to catch up\u2014we need to stay in business in the meantime.\u201d\n\n\u201cPeople want to buy fast, good and cheap,\u201d he said, adding that superior retailers will do that and \u201cthen add something extra\u201d for consumers.\n\nBut he noted that the Boomer generation is no longer landscaping to the level of the past and the younger generations do not yet have enough disposable income to purchase a lot of plant materials.\n\nPlants now have to be more than \u201cpretty\u201d to sell; they have to be essential to someone\u2019s life, Geary said. And the Millennial generation, born between 1980 and 2005, make purchases according to \u201chipness, health, efficiency, gadgets, sustainability/green and experiences.\u201d\n\n\u201cWe\u2019re going to have to produce plants and shopping experiences that meet those needs,\u201d he said.\n\n    Providing ways for people with limited transportation to haul plants back to their homes or setting up mobile trucks similar to what the food industry has done to take products to consumers might be alternatives, he suggested.\n\n\u201cGetting plants into the hands of consumers is more than just an economic issue. It\u2019s also a geographical problem in some areas,\u201d he said.\n\nHe also said that the success of using the Internet for plant sales has not been proven at this point, but pointed to a recent study by Today\u2019s Garden Center magazine which found that only 78 percent of respondents have a website, 74 percent use Facebook, 18 percent use Twitter and less than 10 percent are using Pinterest.\n\n\u201cAnd yet, what generation is the emerging market? And how do you think they receive their information and engage in commerce and community building?\u201d he asked.\n\n    Geary said while the changes in society are impacting the industry, nursery suppliers also are trying to figure out how to match supply with demand.\n\n\u201cHow many purple petunias do we need? With fewer purchases happening, the breeders and growers are asking questions like that,\u201d he said. \u201cThere\u2019s definitely a shift happening at the other end of the supply chain. When the market grows everyone wants a piece; when the market shrinks they have to make tough decisions.\u201d\n\n\u201cCompanies are focusing on only a few of their best selling products and stopping or selling other lines. With the market shrinking, competition is heating up and changing the structure and performance of the industry,\u201d he said.\n-30-\n\n\n\nFind more stories, photos, videos and audio at http://today.agrilife.org", "icon": "https://fbstatic-a.akamaihd.net/rsrc.php/v2/yD/r/aS8ecmYRys0.gif", "type": "link", "id": "107207969434854_296953633747636", "status_type": "shared_story", "description": "Apps for Agriculture workshop slated Oct. 23 in Corpus Christi, Oct. 24 in El CampoCORPUS CHRISTI  \u2013  Workshops conducted by the Texas A&M AgriLife Extension Service to train farmers and ranchers to use their iPhones and iPads to help increase profits are scheduled in Corpus Christi and near Houston..."}

The various speakers at the ARS meeting also emphasized these broad trends when talking about the future of rose cultivars and marketing. The GO GREEN from Ping Lim\u2019s talk was also echoed by Paul Zimmerman, Bill deVor, and my own talk. Although this may not be a major concern in the rose exhibitor\u2019s world as they are adept in the use of chemical protection, the casual gardener does not want to spray repeatedly to protect their roses from disease attacks. Thus the most popular cultivars of roses are resistant to black spot which is our major disease issue we deal with. This resistance also contributes to the convenience of growing roses. The majority of the consumers of roses want a beautiful garden with minimal work. These casual gardeners are also not familiar with specific rose cultivars as are many in the American Rose Society but rather need guidance in selecting roses.
